data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Mos2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_MoS2">MoS<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene">graphene</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Pseudocapacitive+Effect&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_pseudocapacitive%20effect">pseudocapacitive effect</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultrathin+Nanosheets&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultrathin%20nanosheets">ultrathin nanosheets</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn extensive research attention as a promising cost-effective alternative for large-scale energy storage applications in renewable energy and smart grids owing to the abundance of sodium in nature compared to lithium. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> However, the large ionic radius of sodium (Na<sup>+</sup> 1.06 Å vs Li<sup>+</sup> 0.76 Å) and slow reaction kinetics cause volume expansion and large polarization, resulting in low reversible capacities and poor cycling performances. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> As a result, the SIBs’ practical applications have been constrained. It is both crucial and challenging to develop facile and equitable design of electrode materials with high rate performance and long cycle life. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Nanostructured materials, such as metal alloys (Sn, Sb, SnSb), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6−8)</a> transition-metal oxide and sulfides (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> SnS, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> SnS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> WS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> MoS<sub>2</sub>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> and carbonaceous materials Ir/CNFs <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> and Co<sub>0.85</sub>Se@RGO, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> have been investigated to overcome the integral limitations of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and thus to improve the properties of rechargeable SIBs. Among all these nanomaterials, 2D-layered metal dichalcogenides, more specifically MoS<sub>2</sub>, have received serious consideration as a promising SIB anode material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are covalently bonded to form 2D layers, and they are stacked together through weak van der Waals interactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> The large interlayer spacing and weak van der Waals interaction among the layers are beneficial for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and reversible intercalation/extraction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref21" aria-label="reference 19 and 21">(19,21)</a> Nevertheless, due to poor electronic conductivity and slow sodium diffusion kinetics, further applications of MoS<sub>2</sub> as an anode material in SIBs are limited. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> To overcome these problems, a variety of approaches have been proposed, including reducing the particle size to the nanometer scale, diminishing the diffusion path of Na<sup>+</sup>, and mixing MoS<sub>2</sub> with conductive agents to improve the electron conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref17 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref17 ref25" aria-label="reference 14, 17 and 25">(14,17,25)</a> The reduction in particle size further increases the specific surface area of the entire electrode, enabling the electrolyte to moisten the electrode materials properly and subsequently to boost the transport kinetics of the electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The electronic conductivity can be enhanced noticeably by adding carbon materials such as carbon nanotubes (CNTs), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> carbon nanofibers (CNFs), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref29" aria-label="reference 17 and 29">(17,29)</a> carbon sphere, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a> and graphene. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> These materials can also accommodate the volume changes of MoS<sub>2</sub>, thus refining the rate capability and cycling stability of the SIB electrode. Although a spectrum of strategies has been attempted, it is yet thought-provoking to develop stable MoS<sub>2</sub>–graphene-based composite electrode materials with highly conductive channels for efficient electron transfer. For energy storage devices, it is always attractive to use 3D rather than 2D nanostructures because 3D nanostructures have less aggregation and agglomeration and thus can sustain the superior intrinsic characteristics of nanomaterials, such as high surface area, unique physical properties, and structural stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 35 to 37">(35−37)</a> Inspired by natural marigold flowers, we obtained ultrathin MoS<sub>2</sub>-G networks by synthesizing MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflowers and then wrapped these prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> flowery structures with layered graphene. The prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>-G hybrid networks have high electronic conductivity and act as efficient conductive channels for electrons. MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, mounted into the hierarchical morphology of the MFs, could reduce the diffusion distance of sodium ions and provide them large active surface sites. During the processes of charging and discharging, the hierarchical flowery morphology and layered graphene wrapping could accommodate the MoS<sub>2</sub> volume change. The increased MoS<sub>2</sub> interlayer spacing and layer-to-layer surface contact between MoS<sub>2</sub> and graphene, revealed in SEM and TEM observations, would make diffusion and reversible sodium ion intercalation/extraction easier and at the same time would reduce the interface impedance. The 3D MoS<sub>2</sub>-G prepared conductive network of ultrathin microstructures showed superior electrochemical performance, including high specific capacity and improved structural stability as an anode material in SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Commercially available analytical grade chemicals without further processing were used.</div><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Preparation of Marigold Flower-Inspired MoS<sub>2</sub> 3D Microstructures</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Marigold flower-like MoS<sub>2</sub> was synthesized as defined in the reported work with appropriate optimization to make MoS<sub>2</sub> NSs few-layer and bigger in size to shape the final assembly as a 3D microstructure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> In the typical synthesis process, 0.6 g of sodium molybdate dihydrate (Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O) and 0.9 g of thiourea (CH<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>S) powders were dissolved in 20 mL of deionized water followed by 15 min of ultrasonication. Then, 0.05 g of poly(ethylene glycol) (PEG-1000) was gradually added with vigorous stirring for 30 min. In a dried 30 mL Teflon-lined stainless autoclave, the resulting solution was transferred under ambient conditions, and the hydrothermal treatment was carried out at 200 ° C for 15 h. After the reaction, the solution was cooled to room temperature. The black MoS<sub>2</sub> precipitate was collected, washed with Millipore water and absolute ethanol, and subsequently vacuum dried at 90 °C for 12 h.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Synthesis of Graphene-Wrapped MoS<sub>2</sub> (MoS<sub>2</sub>-G)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Layered graphene oxide (GO) was synthesized by the modified Hummers method from powder graphite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Then, graphene-wrapped MoS<sub>2</sub> (MoS<sub>2</sub>-G) hybrid microstructures were synthesized by a facile liquid-phase droplet method followed by hydrothermal treatment. In a typical synthesis process, 0.1 g of as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> particles was dispersed in 20 mL of deionized water followed by 30 min of ultrasonication. Then, 10 mL of GO aqueous solution (1 mg·mL<sup>–1</sup>) was prepared. After ultrasonication for 1 h, the prepared GO solution was added dropwise into the aforementioned MoS<sub>2</sub> aqueous dispersion under magnetic stirring followed by ultrasonication for 30 min. Thereafter, the resulting brownish-black solution was heated to 150 °C in a 50 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave for 12 h. Then, the black semigel precipitate produced was centrifuged at 7000 rpm for 8 min, collected from the residual, and washed several times with distilled water and ethanol followed by drying in a vacuum oven at 80 °C. Then, the dried product was heated up to 400 °C at a heating rate of 3 °C·min<sup>–1</sup> in an Ar atmosphere with a flow rate of 30 mL·min<sup>–1</sup> in a tabular furnace to produce the desired graphene-wrapped MoS<sub>2</sub> hybrid microstructure.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Morphological and Structural Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The detailed microstructures and morphology of the prepared samples were investigated with a field emission scanning electron microscope (JEOL JSM-7610F FEG-SEM) and a transmission electron microscope (TEM, FEI Titan G2 80-300 ST). To obtain high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images at 80 kV, a monochromator and image corrector were operated. A Bruker D8 Advance X-ray diffractometer with standard Cu Kα radiation was used to characterize the crystal structure and phase purity of the prepared samples. The MoS<sub>2</sub> and graphene content in the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G composite was measured by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA), which was performed with a simultaneous thermal analyzer (Netzsch STA449F3, Germany) under N<sub>2</sub> gas flow at 20 mL·min<sup>–1</sup> at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in the temperature range of 30–750 °C. Raman spectroscopic analysis was carried out under 532 nm laser light on a confocal Raman microscopy spectrometer (WITec alpha300 R). To investigate the primary composition and chemical status of the prepared samples, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis was conducted on a Perkin-Elmer PHI 5300 ESCA system (Mg Kα) at 250 W under a vacuum greater than 10<sup>–6</sup> Pa. The binding energies in all XPS spectra were calibrated referenced to the C 1s peak at 284.6 eV.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Electrochemical Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical measurements of the as-synthesized MoS<sub>2</sub>-G were tested in CR2032 coin cells with sodium foil as the counter electrode. The active material (70 wt %), sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (10 wt %), and 20 wt % conductive carbon black in deionized water were used to fabricate the working electrode. The active material loading was 1.2–1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The cell was vacuum-packed with a glass fiber membrane separator (Whatman, GF/D) and ester-based electrolyte (1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate, diethyl carbonate, and fluoroethylene carbonate with volume ratio of 0.475:0.475:0.05) in an argon-filled glovebox. The galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were carried out on a LAND instrument at room temperature. To measure the CV and EIS in the voltage range of 0.01 and 3.0 V, a CHI660E electrochemical workstation was used. Meanwhile, the electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) were captured in the frequency range of 100,000–0.01 Hz.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.1.  Morphological and Structural Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">In well-established solid-state methods, a conservative dispersion of mixing graphene with nanoparticles typically requires large quantities of graphene, which simply yields a malicious agglomeration and stacking, frequently resulting in an inhomogeneous and inadequate product. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 39 to 41">(39−41)</a> Herein, we have developed a facile synthesis route to obtain MoS<sub>2</sub>-G microarchitectures by chemical wrapping of graphene on the as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs, as shown schematically in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>. In a typical synthesis, we obtained the MoS<sub>2</sub> MF structure by scheming a reaction with a controlled concentration of the MoS<sub>2</sub> precursor and thiourea. Generally, the synthesis process of MoS<sub>2</sub> is initiated by nucleation and growth, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> and then the further growth of MoS<sub>2</sub> crystallites turns the particles into sheets. These NSs are then shaped into a microflower assembly by hydrothermal heat treatment. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> In our study, a scalable pathway is developed by adjusting the concentration of thiourea and the precursor such that the defect-induced MF morphology can be achieved. Surplus thiourea not only played a role as a reductant to reduce Mo(VI) to Mo(IV) but also acted as an efficient stabilizer for the ultrathin nanosheet morphology. Thus, excess thiourea can be adsorbed on the surface of primary nanocrystallites, leading to the formation of a defect-induced structure. Graphene oxide (GO) was prepared by the modified Hummers method, and the as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs were then chemically treated with graphene oxide droplets at optimized temperature followed by calcination to get the desired MoS<sub>2</sub>-G networks.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/medium/am-2019-042607_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0007.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic diagram of the synthesis of the graphene-wrapped marigold flower-inspired 3D MoS<sub>2</sub> ultrathin microstructures (corresponding insets are TEM and SEM images showing the morphology of the prepared samples).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b04260&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The SEM images of prepared samples are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows that the samples consist of uniform marigold-shaped MF architectures with diameters ranging from 2 to 3 μm. The inset digital photograph shows a natural marigold flower. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b illustrates the well-defined 3D microstructure of a single MoS<sub>2</sub> MF with broad interface spaces. The MF is composed of several ultrathin nanosheets (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c) sporadically and arbitrarily arranged. A magnified TEM image of these NSs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) discloses that these NSs are a few layers of MoS<sub>2</sub> ultrathin sheets with a thickness of 2–3 nm, which is later confirmed by the HRTEM analysis. These MoS<sub>2</sub> ultrathin NSs (∼2 nm) tend to assembled into a wide hierarchical sphere, considered to be a self-assembly of MoS<sub>2</sub> shaped into a marigold flower-like morphology. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a> displays the uniform MoS<sub>2</sub> MF structure at the large scale. It is difficult to find a single free MoS<sub>2</sub> flake or sheet throughout the large-scale SEM image of the as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs, revealing the controlled and homogeneous nature of the synthesis strategy. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e,f displays the SEM images of the graphene-wrapped MoS<sub>2</sub>-G hybrid networks at different magnifications. Bare MoS<sub>2</sub> MF particles are scarcely found in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>, which indicates that the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers are well-wrapped and covered by graphene-layered sheets. Moreover, to figure out the existence and distribution of Mo and S in the MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs and to confirm that the structure of the spatial MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs is effectively wrapped and covered with uniform distribution of graphene in the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G composite, energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) analysis was performed to acquire the elemental maps. As shown by the results in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, the Mo, S, and C elements are homogeneously dispersed in the MF structures, and the obtained MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs are well-wrapped and homogeneously covered by the graphene layer. The precise content of MoS<sub>2</sub> and graphene in the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G composite was calculated using TGA (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The weight loss for the pristine MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs is about 17.04% over the temperature range from 30 to 750 °C, while the weight loss of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G composite is 23.75%. Therefore, the weight ratio of MoS<sub>2</sub> and graphene in the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G composite was estimated to be 93 and 7 wt %, respectively. These 3D microarchitectures are striking because the internal free space is capable of accommodating the volume variation during charging/discharging, and 2 nm ultrathin nanosheets make fast transport of Na<sup>+</sup> ions/electrons easier due to their short diffusion length. In addition, the large 3D interconnected graphene networks can help to prevent the self-aggregation of MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs, thereby ensuring the structural integrity and facilitate the effective transfer of electron during cycling by rendering rapid transmission paths.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/medium/am-2019-042607_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0006.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Low-magnification SEM images of the as-prepared marigold-shaped MoS<sub>2</sub> microflower. (b–d) Higher-magnification SEM images highlighting the shape, 3D architecture, and unitary nanosheet thickness of the MoS<sub>2</sub> microflower. (e) High-magnification SEM image of as-synthesized graphene-wrapped MoS<sub>2</sub>-G MF. (f) Low-magnification SEM image of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G MF.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b04260&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To examine the crystal structure and composition of the prepared samples, XRD measurements were carried out. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, the diffraction peaks observed can be indexed to the hexagonal MoS<sub>2</sub> phase (JCPDS 37-1492), which indicates the purity of MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs. As compared to the bare MoS<sub>2,</sub> the weak (002) reflection in MoS<sub>2</sub>-G shows the introduction of graphene in MoS<sub>2</sub>, which significantly prevents the agglomeration and restacking of MoS<sub>2</sub> NSs, while the slight expansion of (100) and (110) peaks in diffraction may be due to defect formation along the basal planes of MoS<sub>2</sub> NSs. Notably, the small broad hump around 2θ = 25° is attributed to the presence of graphene albeit in trace amounts. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, the TEM image supports the SEM finding that the synthesized MoS<sub>2</sub>-MFs comprise a combination of ultrathin curved NSs forming the marigold flower-like structure. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c shows the high-magnification TEM image of the flaky structure of MoS<sub>2</sub> sheets at the edge of a single MF. To assure the crystal structures and layer counts along with the lattice spacing, the bright-field HRTEM analysis was performed. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, the HRTEM image of a vertically curved edge shows the typical layered structure of MoS<sub>2</sub>, with an interlayer distance of 0.64 nm, which is slightly greater than the layer-to-layer spacing of 0.61 nm in the bulk MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 45 to 47">(45−47)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e shows the HRTEM top-view image of a single MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet, the <i>d</i> spacings of 0.27 and 0.22 nm are consistent with the (100) and (103) planes of hexagonal MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref48" aria-label="reference 18, 19 and 48">(18,19,48)</a> It is evident from the HRTEM image that the two grains propagate on top of each other in the few-layer MoS<sub>2</sub>, forming a multilayer overlapped region with weak van der Waals forces between the adjacent layers. It is worth noticing that many dislocations and distortions, marked with the red circles in the image, can be observed in the MoS<sub>2</sub> sheet, which suggests a novel defect-induced structure. The defect-induced structure directs the formation of nanosized domains along the basal planes. High-resolution TEM analysis (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f) was also performed to evaluate the quality of the MoS<sub>2</sub> unitary nanosheet on the atomic scale. The ultrathin sheet is apparently continuous in the low-magnification TEM image, whereas the HRTEM image shows that it has a single crystalline hexagonal atomic structure. Upon careful observation, the disordered atomic arrangements are seen, which cause the cracks and defects in the basal planes, consequently resulting in the addition of Na<sup>+</sup> storage active sites. In conclusion, due to the special defect-induced structure, supplementary active sites can be exposed, which is advantageous for the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process. In short, the few-layer MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets along with the graphene wrapping provide surplus open stations for sodium ion storage. This distinct architecture enhances the electrical conductivity and stability with excellent sodium storage performance of the active materials.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/medium/am-2019-042607_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns of GO, MoS<sub>2</sub> MF, and MoS<sub>2</sub>-G MF hybrid network. (b, c) Low-resolution TEM. (d–f) High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of MoS<sub>2</sub>-MF nanosheets at different scales.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b04260&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The presence of MoS<sub>2</sub> and graphene in the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G MF network was investigated by Raman analysis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). The Raman peaks at 383.2 and 410.2 cm<sup>–1</sup> are characteristic peaks of the hexagonal MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs, attributed to E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref49" aria-label="reference 19 and 49">(19,49)</a> The Raman shifts at 379.4 cm<sup>–1</sup> (E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub>) and 408.0 cm<sup>–1</sup> (A<sub>1g</sub>) are credited to the planar (E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup>) and out-of-plane (A<sub>1g</sub>) vibrations in the MoS<sub>2</sub> hybrid matrix, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref50" aria-label="reference 34 and 50">(34,50)</a> indicating the few-layer assembly of crystalline MoS<sub>2</sub> chemically bonded with graphene. The significantly low intensity and comparatively wide peak width of E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> can be credited to the presence of significant defect sites, in good agreement with the HRTEM analysis. A number of coordinately and structurally unsaturated active sulfur atoms are available at these defects sites, which would greatly contribute to the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process. The Raman peaks at ∼1347.8 and 1596 cm<sup>–1</sup> are ascribed to the D and G bands of graphene, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref51" aria-label="reference 45 and 51">(45,51)</a> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis was carried out to examine the oxidation states and interfacial interaction between MoS<sub>2</sub> and graphene in the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G hybrid matrix. The full survey scan spectrum of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G demonstrates the presence of Mo, O, S, and C elements with respect to their corresponding energies (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The high-resolution XPS spectrum of Mo 3d (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b) shows two peaks located at 229.56 and 232.70 eV, corresponding to the binding energies of Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref52" aria-label="reference 42 and 52">(42,52)</a> The separation energy near 3.1 eV is the feature of the Mo species, more specifically the Mo<sup>4+</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub>. Another small peak at 226.7 eV is attributed to S 2s. The peaks at 162.3 and 163.5 eV are ascribed to the coexistence of S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> as shown in the high-resolution XPS spectrum of S 2p (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). The intensity quotient of the typical peaks of approximately 2:1 and a separation energy of ∼1.2 eV are the characteristics of S<sup>2–</sup> species. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Furthermore, after deconvolution, the high-resolution spectrum of C 1s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d) shows three different peaks: typically, C—C/C═C at 284.7 eV and the other two different peaks at 286.0 and 288.5 eV attributed to C–O, C═O and O—C═O, respectively. The C 1s spectrum of GO generally exhibited two main peaks at 284.6 and 286.7 eV corresponding to the binding energy of sp<sup>2</sup> C–C and C–O bonds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> The C 1s spectrum of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G MFs indicated that GO had been totally reduced to graphene as most of the oxygen-containing functional groups were detached during hydrothermal treatment. The results of the XRD and XPS investigations are in agreement with other published works on few-layer MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> All of these XPS results confirm the contact of MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs with wrapped graphene in the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G 3D composite.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/medium/am-2019-042607_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Raman spectra of as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> MF and MoS<sub>2</sub>-G MF 3D network. High-resolution XPS spectra of (b) Mo 3d, (c) S 2p, and (d) C 1s of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G MF hybrid network.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b04260&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.2.  Electrochemical Performances</h3><div class="NLM_p">The outstanding electrochemical performances of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrodes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. The high initial Coulombic efficiency of 81.7% can be obtained at the first cycle, indicating that side reactions occurring at interfaces have been suppressed by the uniform composition of graphene. After 100 cycles, the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode still retained a high capacity of over 500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. During this process, the Coulombic efficiency was maintained at 98%. However, the bare MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode exhibited fast capacity fading and a polarization voltage increase during the initial cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a,b</a>), indicating the positive effect of graphene for enhancing electrical conductivity and relieving volume expansion. Typical discharge–charge curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b) demonstrated slight polarization during the long cycle, indicating high structural and interfacial stability. Rate capability of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode was elevated, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c. When current densities reached 100, 200, 400, 800, and 1600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode exhibited considerable discharge capacities of 606, 555, 507, 438, and 343 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>,respectively. A high charge capacity of 535 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> could be obtained when the rate returned to 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, illustrating the broad Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path and fast Na<sup>+</sup> transfer. Meanwhile, the bare MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode delivered low capacity after cycling at a high rate, indicating the collapse of the crystal structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5c,d</a>). According to the EIS results of MoS<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>-G (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), the outstanding rate performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G can be ascribed to the good interfacial compatibility and fast ion transfer. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d, the platform capacity of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode observably decreased with increasing rates, revealing the sluggish kinetics of conversion reaction. On the other hand, the rate performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G was enhanced by the remarkable pseudocapacitive effect.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/medium/am-2019-042607_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical properties of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrodes. (a) Cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrodes at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Galvanostatic discharge–charge curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrodes. (c) Rate performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrodes at different current densities. (d) Discharge–charge curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrodes at different current densities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b04260&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Meanwhile, the analysis of electrochemical behaviors was explored and is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>. In the first cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a), there is an irreversible reduction peak found at 0.83 V corresponding to the development of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI). In addition, CV curves showed several oxidation/reduction peaks around high voltages of 2.25 and 1.83 V, which can be assigned to the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> On the other hand, the redox peaks at low potentials are attributed to the conversion reaction among Mo, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and MoS<sub>2</sub>. However, the weak currents indicated the incomplete reactions, implying that some of the Na<sub>2</sub>S could be converted into MoS<sub>2</sub>. This process is confirmed by the ex situ XRD patterns, which show the characteristic peaks of S and Na<sub>2</sub>S at fully discharged states (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). Apparently, the oxidation/reduction peaks at 0.79 and 1.74 V are the prominent redox processes, corresponding to the sodiation/desodiation of sulfur. The good symmetry and reproducibility of CV curves demonstrated the highly reversible sodium storage of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/medium/am-2019-042607_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0002.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. In-depth analysis of the electrochemical sodium storage process in MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrodes. (a) CV curves for the first four cycles of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G tested at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) CV curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G at various scan rates from 0.1 to 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Power law relationships between the peak current and scan rate. (d) <i>iv</i><sup>–1/2</sup> vs <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> at different Na extraction potentials around the oxidation peak. (e) CV curves exhibiting the capacitive contribution at 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (f) Capacitive contribution ratios at various scan rates. (g) EIS of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G tested before and after sweeping; the inset is an equivalent circuit diagram of EIS fitting. (h) Schematic illustration of the enabled sodium storage process for MoS<sub>2</sub>-G.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/large/am-2019-042607_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b04260&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">To better investigate the outstanding electrochemical properties of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode, CV curves at different scan rates were determined to attain a reason for dynamics optimization (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). The shape of CV curves gradually became obtuse with increasing rates, which can be ascribed to the enhanced surface diffusion process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c presents the <i>b</i> values calculated from the slope of the plot of log <i>i</i> versus log <i>v</i>, as described in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/medium/am-2019-042607_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>i</i> is the current density, <i>v</i> is the scan rate, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants. Generally, <i>b</i> = 0.5 is the semi-infinite diffusion behavior and <i>b</i> = 1.0 is the capacitive-controlled reaction. The <i>b</i> values for the redox peaks of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode are concentrated on 0.9–1.0, implying that the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode undergoes the typical sodium storage process. Furthermore, the capacitance contribution of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G could be quantitatively distinguished with the help of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/asset/images/medium/am-2019-042607_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i12"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are constants. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> is attained via fitting the gradient of the line relation between <i>i</i>(V)/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> and <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e presents that the intuitive contribution ratio of pseudocapacitance (the cyan region) in contrast with the total current at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> is computed to be 81.7%, indicating that the capacitive-dominant reaction is the subject at this rate. The impact ratio for capacitive-controlled reaction kinetics is strongly dependent on the scan rate, increasing from 74.3% of the total stored charge at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> to 87.7% at 5.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f). Therefore, the superior sodium storage kinetics can be ascribed to the unique designed 3D conductive architecture with a large surface area, which provides a fast transfer of Na<sup>+</sup> and electrons in the MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b04260/suppl_file/am9b04260_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> (Supporting Information) shows the sodium storage performance comparison between the obtained 3D hybrid MoS<sub>2</sub>-G and previously reported MoS<sub>2</sub>-based composites. Meanwhile, the EIS results collected after cycling at various current densities highlight the high interfacial stability of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>g). The charge transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) increased first and then decreased, confirming the development and stabilization of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode. When the SEI layer reached a steady state at a high scan rate, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value was calculated to be 62 Ω. This result reveals that the graphene composition layer can provide more accessible pathways for charge transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. In summary, the enhanced electrochemical performances are mainly derived from the outstanding pseudocapacitive effect and expanded interlayer spacing (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>h). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> These two advantages not only allow fast sodium storage at high current density but also improve the structural stability after insertion of a large amount of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The layer-to-layer interaction between the MoS<sub>2</sub> ultrathin nanosheets and graphene nanosheets activates the sodium storage sites both in the bulk and surface. The special marigold flower-inspired morphology reserves adequate spaces for relieving volume expansion. According to the recent reports on full cells, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59 ref60 ref61 ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref60 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 59 to 62">(59−62)</a> it can be speculated that the prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>-G electrode can be used for the practical application in a real sodium-ion battery. The high initial Coulombic efficiency of ∼80% and outstanding cycling stability meet the key indexes for full cell application.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">Inspired by the marigold flower structures in nature, a simple thiourea-assisted hydrothermal approach has been developed to synthesize the flowery 3D MoS<sub>2</sub> ultrathin structures followed by graphene wrapping to obtain the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G interconnected 3D conductive network. The microscopy characterizations have confirmed that the exposed NS assembly and 3D microarchitecture of MoS<sub>2</sub>-G offer additional active sites, charge diffusion paths, and interface contact area. Moreover, the hierarchically arranged but strongly interconnected 2D ultrathin NSs in 3D MoS<sub>2</sub> MFs combined with the graphene wrapping create strong electrical conductive channels in an electrode. From the electrochemical measurements, it is revealed that the 2 nm ultrathin nanosheets play a vital role in shortening the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion pathways and in the good electrolyte wettability, which ensures high rate performance. In addition, the close contact of 2D ultrathin NSs with graphene empowered the superior electrochemical performance for its use as an anode material of SIBs. Such a unique MoS<sub>2</sub>-G 3D architecture influenced by the surface-to-surface intimate contact between MoS<sub>2</sub> and graphene effectively improves the electron/ion transport kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub> and ensures the structural integrity, resulting in superior electrochemical performance. All of the above discussed results show that the developed marigold flower-shape MoS<sub>2</sub>-G with its unmatched 3D morphology and new architecture has the potential to be adapted for designing high-performance anode materials for low-cost and large-scale applications in SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljsoLJH8OASxw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: Present and Future</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljsoLJH8OASxw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201410376"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kundu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Talaie, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duffort, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nazar, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of some of the current scientific issues underpinning sodium-ion batteries.  Cathode and anode materials and electrolytes for high-performance sodium-ion batteries as well as computational studies of sodium ion mobility are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrXjTRqIj7rwrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljsoLJH8OASxw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXjslCiurs%253D&amp;md5=ef99bad4dc7a644dfa380894720626ed</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600943"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, M.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">From Lithium-Ion to Sodium-Ion Batteries: Advantages, Challenges, and Surprises</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nayak, Prasant Kumar; Yang, Liangtao; Brehm, Wolfgang; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">57</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mobile and stationary energy storage by rechargeable batteries is a topic of broad societal and economical relevance.  Lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. is at the forefront of the development, but a massively growing market will likely put severe pressure on resources and supply chains.  Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been reconsidered with the aim of providing a lower-cost alternative that is less susceptible to resource and supply risks.  On paper, the replacement of lithium by sodium in a battery seems straightforward at first, but unpredictable surprises are often found in practice.  What happens when replacing lithium by sodium in electrode reactions.  This review provides a state-of-the art overview on the redox behavior of materials when used as electrodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries, resp.  Advantages and challenges related to the use of sodium instead of lithium are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrGeYJemvxIpbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgRrtgnrCn5mg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhvVKmurvE&amp;md5=4342a80be51730e08dc9faec2f79fb63</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.03.029"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Darwiche, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sougrati, M.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin-coated viral nanoforests as sodium-ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yihang; Xu, Yunhua; Zhu, Yujie; Culver, James N.; Lundgren, Cynthia A.; Xu, Kang; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Designed as a high-capacity alloy host for Na-ion chem., a forest of Sn nanorods with a unique core-shell structure was synthesized on viral scaffolds, which were genetically engineered to ensure a nearly vertical alignment upon self-assembly onto a metal substrate.  The interdigital spaces thus formed between individual rods effectively accommodated the vol. expansion and contraction of the alloy upon sodiation/desodiation, while addnl. carbon-coating engineered over these nanorods further suppressed Sn aggregation during extended electrochem. cycling.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Better Cycling Performances of Bulk Sb in Na-Ion Batteries Compared to Li-Ion Systems: An Unexpected Electrochemical Mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Darwiche, Ali; Marino, Cyril; Sougrati, Moulay T.; Fraisse, Bernard; Stievano, Lorenzo; Monconduit, Laure</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">134</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">51</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">20805-20811</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure micrometric antimony can be successfully used as neg. electrode material in Na-ion batteries, sustaining a capacity close to 600 mAh g-1 at a high rate with a Coulombic efficiency of 99 over 160 cycles, an extremely high capacity compared to any other compd. tested against both Li and Na.  The reaction mechanism with Na does not simply go through the alloying mechanism obsd. for Li where the intermediate species are those expected from the phase diagram.  In the case of Na, the intermediate phases are mostly amorphous and could not be precisely identified.  Surprisingly, it is evidenced that a competition takes place at the end of the discharge of the Sb/Na cell between the formation of the hexagonal and the cubic polymorphs of Na3Sb, the last being described in the literature as unstable at atm. pressure and only synthesized under high pressure (1-9 GPa).  In addn., fluoroethylene carbonate added to the electrolyte combined with an appropriate electrode formulation based on CM-cellulose, carbon black, and vapor ground carbon fibers seems to be determinant in the excellent performances of this material.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpfZ6rvnJyfxrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgRrtgnrCn5mg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhslCgsbnE&amp;md5=75362e7708fe6434655290ce0aa7cac0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA02165H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Microorganism-Moulded Pomegranate-Like Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C Nanocomposite for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Microorganism-moulded pomegranate-like Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanocomposite for advanced sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Qizhen; Chang, Xiaqing; Sun, Ning; Liu, Huan; Chen, Renjie; Wu, Feng; Xu, Bin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9982-9990</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) with a NASICON crystal structure is a promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries; however, it has a low rate performance due to its poor elec. cond.  Herein, pomegranate-like NVP/C composites were proposed and prepd. via a simple and cost-efficient method using yeast as the mold.  Owing to the strong adsorption ability of yeast, high tolerance to extreme conditions and high nitrogen and phosphorus content, a hierarchically structured material composed by NVP particles embedded within a N-/P-doped carbon framework was formed in situ.  In the NVP/C composites, the nanoscaled NVP grains coated by carbon, derived from the cytoplasm, and micron-sized carbon capsules, which resulted from the carbonization of the sturdy cell walls, were formed to further accommodate dozens of the carbon-coated NVP grains, resulting in a pomegranate-like architecture.  This unique structure and the N-/P-doped carbon framework can provide superior electrochem. kinetics and stability, with efficient electron pathways, and can also buffer vol. changes during Na+ insertion/extn.  As a result, the NVP/C composites exhibit a good rate performance (113.9 mA h g-1 at 10C) and an outstanding long-term cycling stability (capacity retention of around 74.7% after 10 000 cycles).  The properties of the pomegranate-like structure molded by yeast microorganisms are remarkable and the NVP/C composite is believed to be a promising electrode material for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpQzdjVQZC9k7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgRrtgnrCn5mg"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Confining SnS2 Ultrathin Nanosheets in Hollow Carbon Nanostructures for Efficient Capacitive Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yuhan; Yu, Xin-Yao; Fang, Yongjin; Zhu, Xiaoshu; Bao, Jianchun; Zhou, Xiaosi; Lou, Xiong Wen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Joule</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin disulfide (SnS2) is a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries because of its high specific capacity.  However, the low cond. and large vol. change during reaction with Na+ ions greatly limit its practical application.  Herein, a multistep templating method has been exploited for the rational design and synthesis of SnS2 nanosheets confined in carbon nanotubes (SnS2@CNTs).  To demonstrate the universality of this method, SnS2 nanosheets confined in carbon nanoboxes (SnS2@CNBs) and hollow carbon nanospheres (SnS2@CNSs) have also been synthesized by simply changing the template in the reaction system.  Due to their unique structural merits, the SnS2@CNTs, SnS2@CNBs, and SnS2@CNSs show improved sodium storage performance in terms of high specific capacity, good cycling stability, and superior rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrVwzLo2TpkkLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljgAFHuL5G9TQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXpsFOntb8%253D&amp;md5=d4ed81f05f770b71bec72fe2d656c008</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.6b12692"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Si, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Few-Layer SnS<sub>2</sub>/Reduced Graphene Oxide Sandwich Hybrid for Efficient Sodium Storage</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Few-Layer SnS2/Reduced Graphene Oxide Sandwich Hybrid for Efficient Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tu, Fengzhang; Xu, Xin; Wang, Pengzi; Si, Ling; Zhou, Xiaosi; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">121</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3261-3269</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries have lately received considerable attention as an alternative to lithium-ion batteries because sodium resources are essentially inexhaustible and ubiquitous around the world.  Despite recent reports on cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries have shown electrochem. activities close to their lithium-ion counterparts, the major scientific challenge for sodium-ion batteries is to exploit efficient anode materials.  Herein, we demonstrate that a hybrid material composed of few-layer SnS2 nanosheets sandwiched between reduced graphene oxide (RGO) nanosheets exhibits a high specific capacity of 843 mAh/g (calcd. based on the mass of SnS2 only) at a c.d. of 0.1 A/g and a 98% capacity retention after 100 cycles when evaluated between 0.01 and 2.5 V.  Employing ex situ high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy and selected area electron diffraction techniques, we illustrate the high specific capacity of our anode through a 3-fold mechanism of intercalation of sodium ions along the ab-plane of SnS2 nanosheets and the subsequent formation of Na2S2 and Na15Sn4 through conversion and alloy reactions.  The existence of RGO nanosheets in the hybrid material functions as a flexible backbone and high-speed electronic pathways, guaranteeing that an appropriate resilient space buffers the anisotropic dilation of SnS2 nanosheets along the ab-plane and c-axis for stable cycling performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp7u2__bTlgVbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljgAFHuL5G9TQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXitVCjtrg%253D&amp;md5=8512fccffb30789680f4d2b2a62843db</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201601057"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kong, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sim, G.
